# Introduction

This document describes a simulation study of a linear regression model
with an intercept, a slope and an error term. First, the model is used
to generate multiple datasets which are subsequently analysed to
estimate the model’s parameters via maximum likelihood. Various
estimation methods are used and compared. All methods result in the same
estimates but some are computationally faster than others. The document
uses the package *tidyverse*.

``` r
library(tidyverse)
```

# Functions

The following function generates data for a linear regression model. The
subsequent functions estimate the parameters which best fit these data
assuming the same model. This is done using multiple estimation methods
which all result in the same maximum likelihood estimates.

``` r
generate.data <- function(n, intercept, slope, sd.error) {
  x <- rnorm(n)
  y <- intercept + slope * x + sd.error * rnorm(n)
  data <- data.frame(
    n = n,
    intercept = intercept,
    slope = slope,
    sd.error = sd.error,
    x = x,
    y = y
  )
  return(data)
}
```

The following estimation method makes use of the analytical solution of
ordinary least squares.

``` r
estimate.model.and.get.coefficients.ols <- function(data) {
  ptm <- proc.time()
  x <- data$x
  y <- data$y
  X <- cbind(1, x)
  XtX_inv <- solve(t(X) %*% X)
  beta_hat <- XtX_inv %*% t(X) %*% y
  sd <- sd(y - X %*% beta_hat)
  time <- proc.time() - ptm
  result <- c(
    intercept = beta_hat[[1]],
    slope = beta_hat[[2]],
    sd.error = sd,
    time = time[[3]]
  )
  return(result)
}
```

The following estimation method makes use of the *lm* function.

``` r
estimate.model.and.get.coefficients.lm <- function(data) {
  ptm <- proc.time()
  model <- lm(
    formula = y ~ 1 + x,
    data = data
  )
  time <- proc.time() - ptm
  result <- c(
    intercept = model$coefficients[[1]],
    slope = model$coefficients[[2]],
    sd.error = sd(model$residuals),
    time = time[[3]]
  )
  return(result)
}
```

The following estimation method makes use of the *optim* function.

``` r
estimate.model.and.get.coefficients.optim <- function(data) {
  ptm <- proc.time()
  x <- data$x
  y <- data$y
  model <- optim(
    par = c(0, 0, 0),
    fn = function(par) {
      sum(((par[[1]] + par[[2]] * x - y) / exp(par[[3]])) ^ 2 / 2 + par[[3]])
    }
  )
  time <- proc.time() - ptm
  result <- c(
    intercept = model$par[[1]],
    slope = model$par[[2]],
    sd.error = exp(model$par[[3]]),
    time = time[[3]]
  )
  return(result)
}
```

The following function combines all previous estimation functions to be
able to estimate and compare model parameters on the same input dataset.

``` r
estimate.all.models.and.get.coefficients <- function(data) {
  result <- data.frame(
    method = c("ols", "lm", "optim"),
    rbind(
      estimate.model.and.get.coefficients.ols(data),
      estimate.model.and.get.coefficients.lm(data),
      estimate.model.and.get.coefficients.optim(data)
    )
  )
  return(result)
}
```

# Parameters

The linear regression takes various input parameters. These can all be
varied, one by one, which results in a table of input parameters on
which the simulation study is performed.

``` r
parameters <- tibble(
  parameter.set = seq(5),
  n = c(200, 800, 200, 200, 200),
  intercept = c(1, 1, -0.5, 1, 1),
  slope = c(0.5, 0.5, 0.5, -0.25, 0.5),
  sd.error = c(1, 1, 1, 1, 0.5)
)
parameters
```

| parameter.set |   n | intercept | slope | sd.error |
|--------------:|----:|----------:|------:|---------:|
|             1 | 200 |       1.0 |  0.50 |      1.0 |
|             2 | 800 |       1.0 |  0.50 |      1.0 |
|             3 | 200 |      -0.5 |  0.50 |      1.0 |
|             4 | 200 |       1.0 | -0.25 |      1.0 |
|             5 | 200 |       1.0 |  0.50 |      0.5 |

# Estimation

For each set of input parameters, the generation and estimation
procedure is applied multiple times. Every run results in slightly
different estimates but all methods should produce the same values.
These are compared below.

``` r
estimated.parameters <- parameters %>%
  expand_grid(
    run = seq(10)
  ) %>%
  reframe(
    generate.data(
      n = n,
      intercept = intercept,
      slope = slope,
      sd.error = sd.error
    ) %>%
      estimate.all.models.and.get.coefficients(),
    .by = c(parameter.set, run)
  )
estimated.parameters
```

# Compare Estimates

The output of the simulation study contains estimated parameters for all
the included methods. These should all give the same result. An easy way
to check this is to plot the estimates of two methods together, one on
the x-axis and one on the y-axis. The points should fall on a 45-degree
line.

``` r
for (model.pairs in combn(unique(estimated.parameters$method), 2, simplify = FALSE)) {
  cat("\n\n## ", model.pairs[[1]], " vs ", model.pairs[[2]], " {.tabset .tabset-pills}\n")
  print(
    ggplot(
      mapping = aes(
        x = !!sym(model.pairs[[1]]),
        y = !!sym(model.pairs[[2]])
      ),
      data = estimated.parameters %>%
        filter(method %in% model.pairs) %>%
        select(-time) %>%
        pivot_longer(c("intercept", "slope", "sd.error")) %>%
        pivot_wider(names_from = "method"),
        alpha = 0.1
    ) +
      geom_point() +
      geom_smooth(
        formula = y ~ x,
        method = "lm",
        col = "green"
      ) +
      geom_abline(
        intercept = 0,
        slope = 1
      ) +
      facet_grid(
        cols = vars(name),
        rows = vars(parameter.set),
        scales = "free"
      )
  )
}
```

# 

All methods give the same result.

# Compare Computation Time

We have seen that all methods produce the same estimates. However, some
methods are computationally faster than others. The table below provides
the mean estimation time.

``` r
estimated.parameters %>%
  group_by(parameter.set, method) %>%
  summarise(
    time = mean(time),
    .groups = "drop"
  ) %>%
  pivot_wider(
    values_from = time,
    names_from = method
  )
```

| parameter.set |    lm |   ols | optim |
|--------------:|------:|------:|------:|
|             1 | 0.002 | 0.003 |     0 |
|             2 | 0.003 | 0.002 |     0 |
|             3 | 0.002 | 0.002 |     0 |
|             4 | 0.001 | 0.002 |     0 |
|             5 | 0.000 | 0.000 |     0 |

The computation times can also be plot as densities.

``` r
ggplot() +
  geom_density(
    mapping = aes(
      x = time,
      col = method
    ),
    data = estimated.parameters
  ) +
  facet_wrap(
    facets = ~ method,
    scales = "free_y"
  )
```

![](N:\Others\Stukjes%20Code\Simulation-Studies\Linear-Regression-Simulation-Study_files/figure-markdown_github/unnamed-chunk-11-1.png)

# Visualise Estimated Parameters

Given that the *lm* method was computationally fast, and that is gave
the same estimates as all other methods, let’s use this to estimate even
more values for generated datasets.

``` r
estimated.parameters <- parameters %>%
  expand_grid(
    run = seq(1000)
  ) %>%
  reframe(
    generate.data(
      n = n,
      intercept = intercept,
      slope = slope,
      sd.error = sd.error
    ) %>%
      estimate.model.and.get.coefficients.lm() %>%
      as_tibble_row(),
    .by = c(parameter.set, run)
  )
estimated.parameters
```

We now visualise the distribution of the estimated parameters, for each
set of the input parameters. We can add a vertical line to indicate the
value of the original, input parameter.

``` r
ggplot() +
  geom_density(
    mapping = aes(
      x = value,
      fill = name
    ),
    data = estimated.parameters %>%
      pivot_longer(
        cols = c("intercept", "slope", "sd.error")
      ),
    alpha = 0.3
  ) +
  geom_vline(
    mapping = aes(
      xintercept = value,
      col = name
    ),
    data = parameters %>%
      pivot_longer(
        cols = c("intercept", "slope", "sd.error")
      )
  ) +
  facet_grid(
    cols = vars(name),
    rows = vars(parameter.set),
    scales = "free"
  )
```

![](N:\Others\Stukjes%20Code\Simulation-Studies\Linear-Regression-Simulation-Study_files/figure-markdown_github/unnamed-chunk-13-1.png)

# Visualise Parameter Correlations

The estimated parameters may also correlate with each other. This is
first visualised by listing the Pearson correlation in a matrix.

``` r
ggplot(
  mapping = aes(
    x = parameter.1,
    y = parameter.2,
    fill = correlation,
    label = sprintf("%.2f", correlation)
  ),
  data = estimated.parameters %>%
    group_by(parameter.set) %>%
    reframe(
      expand_grid(
        parameter.1 = c("intercept", "slope", "sd.error"),
        parameter.2 = c("intercept", "slope", "sd.error")
      ),
      correlation = c(cor(data.frame(intercept, slope, sd.error)))
    )
) +
  geom_tile(alpha = 0.5) +
  geom_text() +
  scale_fill_viridis_c() +
  guides(fill = "none") +
  labs(
    x = NULL,
    y = NULL
  ) +
  facet_wrap(
    facets = vars(parameter.set)
  )
```

![](N:\Others\Stukjes%20Code\Simulation-Studies\Linear-Regression-Simulation-Study_files/figure-markdown_github/unnamed-chunk-14-1.png)
